Critique of the Opponent and the Karaha View
Nasafi criticizes an opponent who severs the link between command and its opposite, and discusses a view that command only implies dislike.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi critique of an opponent (likely Abu Hashim) who denied any relationship between command and its opposite, and the view of a later mutakallim who proposed that command only implies dislike (karaha).

Core Idea
Denying the link between command and its opposite leads to absurd consequences about reward and punishment.

After presenting the main positions, Nasafi turns to refute an opponent (probably Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i) who claimed that command over a thing has no bearing on its opposite. He then reports a later mutakallim's compromise: command merely implies dislike of its opposite. Nasafi ends by questioning this mutakallim's stance on the nature of divine speech, leaving the reader to ponder.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the opponent
Read the part where Nasafi describes the opponent's denial that command over a thing is prohibition of its opposite, and the example of zina and prayer.
Notice the consequences for reward and punishment.
2
Examine the karaha view
Read the later mutakallim's statement that command necessitates dislike (karaha) of the opposite, not prohibition.
This is a middle position.
3
Consider Nasafi's question
Reflect on the final question: does this mutakallim hold the Ahl al-Sunna view of divine speech or the Mu'tazili view?
Nasafi leaves the question open, implying the answer determines consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أصحابنا مع أوائلهم اتفقوا أن كل مأمور به كان تركه وهو فعل مضادة منهيًا عنه، وكل منهي عنه تركه وهو فعل مضادة مأمورا به، إذا كان لكل واحد منهما ترك مخصوص وضد متعين، وكذا عندنا في كل ما له أضداد من الجانين جميعًا، وعندهم فيما له أضداد تقسيم يطول ذكره يعرف ذلك في أصول الفقه، أن عندنا كان الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء أمرًا بضده، إذ كلام الله تعالى عندنا واحد وهو بنفسه أمر بما أمر نهي عما نهى، فكان ما هو الأمر بالشيء نهيًا عن ضده. وعند المعتزلة كلام الله تعالى هذه العبارات المتركبة من الحروف على هذه الصيغات وللأمر صيغة مخصوصة وكذا للنهي صيغة مخصوصة لا يتصور كون النهي أمرًا ولا كون الأمر نهيًا. ولا شك أن ضد المأمور منهي وضد المنهي مأمور. واختلفت عباراتهم في ذلك فزعم بعضهم: أن الأمر بالشيء يدل على النهي عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء يدل على الأمر بضده. وقال بعضهم: الأمر بالشيء يقتضي نهيًا عن ضده وكذا على القلب. ومنهم من يطلق ما يتفق عليه من اللفظ ولا يفرق بين لفظة الدلالة ولفظة الاقتضاء على طريق التساهل. فلما تحير هذا الرجل فى هذه المسألة وتجاهل، وزعم أن ترك الزنا ليس بمأمور به وترك الصلاة ليس بمنهي عنه، زعم أن الأمر بالشيء ليس بنهي عن ضده، ولا يدل عليه ولا يقتضيه، ولا حكم له في الضد، ويبقى الضد على ما كان قبل ورود الأمر، وكذا في جانب النهي. وزعم أن ثواب من انعدم منه الزنا لا بمقابلة فعل ضده، وعقاب من انعدمت منه الصلاة لا بمقابلة فعل ضده. ثم إن بعض المتكلمين ممن تكلم في أصول الفقه من أهل ديارنا ذكر أني أقول: إن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده لا نهيه ولا أقول إنه نهى عن ضده ولا أقول إنه يدل على نهيه ولا أنه يقتضي نهيًا. ولست أدري ماذا كان رأيه في الكلام؟ أنه هو المعنى القائم بالذات المنافي للسكوت والآفة وهو بعينه أمر بما أمر به نهي عما نهى عنه كما هو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة. أو هو هذه الحروف المنظومة والأصوات المقطوعة، وما كان أمرًا لا يكون نهيًا، وما كان نهيًا لا يكون أمرًا كما هو مذهب المعتزلة؟ وكذا ما كان رأيه أن توجه الوعيد
Then indeed our companions, along with their early authorities, agreed that every commanded thing — its abandonment, which is the performance of its opposite, is prohibited; and every prohibited thing — its abandonment, which is the performance of its opposite, is commanded, since each of them has a specific abandonment and a determinate opposite. Likewise according to us in everything that has opposites from both sides; and according to them regarding what has opposites there is a classification whose mention is lengthy, known in usul al-fiqh. That according to us, command over a thing is prohibition of its opposite, and prohibition over a thing is command of its opposite, since according to us the speech of God Most High is one, and He Himself commands what He commands and prohibits what He prohibits, so that which is command over a thing is prohibition of its opposite. According to the Mu'tazila, the speech of God Most High is these expressions composed of letters in these forms, and the command has a specific form, and likewise the prohibition has a specific form; it is inconceivable that the prohibition be a command or the command be a prohibition. And there is no doubt that the opposite of the commanded is prohibited and the opposite of the prohibited is commanded. And their expressions differed regarding that: some claimed that the command over a thing indicates (yadullu) the prohibition of its opposite, and the prohibition over a thing indicates the command of its opposite. Others said: the command over a thing necessitates (yaqtadi) a prohibition of its opposite, and vice versa. And among them are those who use whatever wording is agreed upon, and do not distinguish between the word 'indication' and the word 'necessitation' by way of laxity. When this man became confused in this matter and feigned ignorance, and claimed that abandoning zina is not commanded and abandoning prayer is not prohibited, he claimed that command over a thing is not a prohibition of its opposite, nor does it indicate it, nor necessitate it, nor does it have any ruling concerning the opposite; the opposite remains as it was before the command came, and likewise regarding prohibition. And he claimed that the reward of one from whom zina is absent is not in exchange for performing its opposite, and the punishment of one from whom prayer is absent is not in exchange for performing its opposite. Then one of the mutakallimun who spoke in usul al-fiqh from the people of our region mentioned that I say: the command over a thing necessitates dislike (karaha) of its opposite, not its prohibition; I do not say it is a prohibition of its opposite, nor do I say it indicates its prohibition, nor that it necessitates a prohibition. I do not know what his opinion was concerning speech: is it the meaning subsisting in the essence, contrary to silence and incapacity, which is itself command of what He commands and prohibition of what He prohibits, according to the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a? Or is it these arranged letters and articulated sounds, and what was a command is not a prohibition and what was a prohibition is not a command, according to the madhhab of the Mu'tazila? And likewise, what was his opinion concerning the direction of the threat...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage classifies and explains the relationship between command (amr) and its opposite (didd), and between prohibition (nahy) and its opposite, according to the Maturidi school (our companions) and the Mu'tazila. It begins by stating the consensus of the Maturidi scholars that every commanded act has a specific opposite; abandoning the commanded (i.e., performing its opposite) is prohibited, and abandoning the prohibited (i.e., performing its opposite) is commanded. This is because, for Ahl al-Sunna, God's speech is one single attribute (al-kalam al-nafsi) that is both commanding and prohibiting simultaneously. For the Mu'tazila, speech is composed of separate created utterances, so a command cannot be a prohibition. The passage then notes the different Mu'tazili formulations: some say command indicates (yadullu) prohibition of its opposite; others say it necessitates (yaqtadi) it; some use the terms loosely. The opponent (Abu Hashim) is criticized for denying any relationship between command and its opposite, and for claiming that reward for avoiding sin is not due to performing its opposite. Then a later mutakallim from the author's region is quoted as saying that command only implies dislike (karaha) of the opposite, not prohibition. The author questions this mutakallim's stance on the nature of divine speech: does he hold the Ahl al-Sunna view (single meaning) or the Mu'tazili view (composed letters)? The passage ends with an incomplete remark about 'the direction of the threat'.

Key Terms

ثواب
عقاب
معنى قائم بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That command over a thing has absolutely no relation to its opposite; this is the opponent's view.
Correction: Nasafi shows that this leads to problematic consequences and is rejected by Ahl al-Sunna.

Checkpoint
What consequence does Nasafi draw from the opponent's denial that command relates to its opposite?
A) That all actions become permissible.
B) That reward for avoiding sin is not due to performing its opposite.
C) That punishment becomes unjust.
D) That command and prohibition become identical.

Key Takeaway
Denying any link between command and its opposite leads to unacceptable theological consequences; the Maturidi view maintains the link through the unity of divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that simply avoiding a sin (like backbiting) automatically earns you reward, without actively doing the opposite good (like defending someone's honor)? How does Nasafi's critique of the opponent's claim on reward challenge that assumption? Will you change today how you view your avoidance of sins?
?
When you miss a prayer, do you console yourself that it was just an omission and not a sin? This passage shows that the opponent's view leads to no punishment for omitting prayer. Yet you know punishment is real. How does this realization press you to take every commanded act more seriously, knowing its opposite is not neutral? What one step will you take to ensure you never treat a command as optional?
?
The passage contrasts two views of divine speech: one unified and one fragmented. If God's command and prohibition are actually one reality, how does that reshape your understanding of obedience? Does it mean that when you obey one command, you are simultaneously avoiding a prohibition? How will you live today with that unified purpose?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's Verse-Based Proof
Nasafi refutes verses used to prove capacity precedes action and interprets 'خذوا ما آتاكم بقوة'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verse 'خذوا ما آتاكم بقوة' actually supports the Ash'ari-Maturidi position that capacity is contemporaneous with the action, not prior.

Core Idea
Capacity must exist at the time of the action, not before it.

The Mu'tazila claimed that certain Qur'anic verses prove capacity precedes the action. Nasafi refutes this by reinterpreting those verses and using the verse 'خذوا ما آتاكم بقوة' as evidence. In this lesson, we examine his argument and see how the verse actually proves simultaneity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Nasafi's argument.
Notice the brevity and logical precision of the text.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila's claim
The Mu'tazila used verses that seem to indicate capacity (istita'a) precedes the action. Nasafi begins by reinterpreting these as referring to the first capacity (safety of causes and instruments).
3
Analyze Nasafi's proof from the verse
Focus on the verse 'Take what We have given you with strength'. Nasafi argues that 'strength' must exist at the time of taking; otherwise it would not be a taking with strength. This shows capacity must be simultaneous.
Consider the analogy of taking with the hand.
4
Examine the logical argument about persistence
Nasafi adds that if capacity preceded the action, it would have to persist until the action, but capacity is an accident that cannot persist. Therefore it cannot precede.
This addresses the philosophical impossibility of accidents enduring.
5
Consider the alternative exegesis
Some exegetes interpret 'strength' as earnestness and perseverance, which further supports the idea that capacity is contemporaneous rather than prior.
This shows the verse does not necessarily imply temporal precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما تتعلق به المعتزلة من الآيات التي فيها إشارة إلى تقدم الاستطاعة، محمول على الاستطاعة الأولى التي هي سلامة الأسباب وصحة الآلات، وقوله تعالى: (خُذُوا مَا آتَيْنَاكُم بِقُوَّةٍ) دليلنا؛ لأنه إنما يصير أخذا بالقوة إذا كانت القوة وقت الأخذ موجودة، فأما إذا كانت هي معدومة وقت الأخذ فلا يكون أخذا بقوة كالأخذ باليد، ثم ليس من ضرورة الأخذ بالقوة تقدمها على الأخذ بل وجودها عند الأخذ، ومن ضرورة وجودها عند الأخذ أن لا تكون موجودة قبله لاستحالة بقائها. على أن أهل التأويل قالوا: (خُذُوا مَا آتَيْنَاكُم بِقُوَّةٍ) أي: بجد ومواظبة.
What the Mu'tazila cling to from the verses that contain an indication of the precedence of capacity is to be interpreted as referring to the first capacity, which is the safety of causes and soundness of instruments. And His statement the Exalted: 'Take what We have given you with strength' is our proof; because one only becomes a taker with strength if the strength exists at the time of taking. As for when it is non-existent at the time of taking, then it is not a taking with strength, like taking with the hand. Moreover, it is not necessary for taking with strength that the strength precede the taking, rather its existence at the time of taking; and it is necessary by its existence at the time of taking that it not have existed before it, due to the impossibility of its persistence. Moreover, the people of interpretation said: 'Take what We have given you with strength', meaning: with seriousness and perseverance.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's proof from verses that apparently indicate that capacity (istita'a) precedes the action. The author first states that such verses are to be interpreted as referring to the first capacity (al-istita'a al-ula), which is the safety of causes and soundness of instruments. Then he brings a proof from the Qur'anic verse "Take what We have given you with strength" (al-A'raf 7:171). He argues that the word "strength" must exist at the time of taking; otherwise, it is not a taking with strength. This shows that capacity must be present at the time of the action, not before. Furthermore, he points out that if the capacity were prior, it would have to persist until the action, but the capacity for the action is an accident which cannot persist, so it cannot precede. Finally, he notes an alternate interpretation by exegetes who interpret "strength" as earnestness and persistence, which further supports the idea that capacity is contemporaneous.

Key Terms
استطاعة
قدرة
قوة
تقدم
بقاء
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'خذوا ما آتاكم بقوة' proves capacity precedes the action.
Correction: The verse actually proves capacity must be present at the time of the action, not before.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, how does the verse 'خذوا ما آتاكم بقوة' support the position that capacity is simultaneous with the action?
A) It implies strength must precede the taking.
B) It implies strength must exist at the time of taking.
C) It implies strength is not needed.
D) It implies strength is a reward.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'خذوا ما آتاكم بقوة' shows that capacity must be present at the time of the action, not before.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'Take what We have given you with strength', do you truly believe your strength to obey Allah comes only at the moment of action, not from your own stored reserve? How does this affect your reliance on Allah versus your own planning?
?
If capacity to act is an accident that cannot persist, what does that imply about your need for Allah's constant creation of your actions? Are you living with that awareness each moment?
?
The verse commands taking with strength at the time of taking. In which areas of your life are you delaying obedience, waiting for some future capacity that may never come? What one step can you take right now with the strength Allah gives you at this moment?

Answering the Objection of Impossible Obligation
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila's objection about simultaneous capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that simultaneous capacity leads to obligation of what is beyond capacity, by arguing that capacity can be suitable for both opposites.

Core Idea
Capacity for disbelief is also suitable for faith, so the command to believe is never impossible.

The Mu'tazila argue that if capacity were simultaneous with the act, a non-believer would lack capacity for faith, making the command to believe an impossible obligation. Nasafi responds by pointing out that the very capacity that produces unbelief is also capable of producing faith. Thus, the objection fails. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the beginning where Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila's argument: if capacity is not prior, the unbeliever has no capacity for faith, so commanding faith is impossible.
Note the Mu'tazila's premise that capacity must precede action.
2
Analyze Nasafi's first response
Examine how he invokes the view that capacity is suitable for both opposites. The unbeliever's capacity could have been used for faith, so he was capable of believing.
This is the key counter-argument.
3
See the refutation of the Mu'tazila's own position
Nasafi turns the tables: the Mu'tazila themselves must hold that the obligation is to replace disbelief with faith, which requires capacity for either opposite, contradicting their own stance.
This shows the objection is internally inconsistent.
4
Conclusion
Note the final statement: the objection is invalid. Understand why the Maturidi position is consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم : إن الاستطاعة لو لم تكن سابقة على الفعل لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق وهو قبيح، وهذا لأن قدرة الإيمان لو كانت مع الإيمان ولا إيمان مع الكافر فلا تكون له قدرة الإيمان، فكان تكليفه الإيمان تكليف ما لا يطاق.

فنقول لهم: إن من قال من أصحابنا إن القدرة تصلح للضدين فهو يقول: إن قدرة الكفر كانت صالحة للإيمان فمنحه القدرة الصالحة للإيمان، فكان يجب عليه أن يكتسب الإيمان بدل الكفر.

والمعتزلة لا يمكنهم إلا هذا إذ الواجب عليهم إيجاد الإيمان بدلاً من الكفر فأما إيجاده مع قيام الكفر فمحال، فكان هو ممكنا بأن يفعل بالقدرة الصالحة للضدين الإيمان لا الكفر، فكذا هؤلاء، فكان الإلزام على هذا فاسداً.
And as for their statement: If capacity were not prior to the act, it would involve obligation of what is beyond capacity (taklif ma la yutaq), which is repugnant. This is because if the capacity for faith were with faith, and there is no faith with the unbeliever, then he has no capacity for faith, so obligating him to faith is obligation of what is beyond capacity.

We say to them: Whoever among our companions says that capacity is suitable for both opposites (salih li-l-diddain) says: The capacity for disbelief was suitable for faith, so he was granted a capacity suitable for faith, so it was incumbent upon him to acquire faith instead of disbelief.

And the Mu'tazila cannot but accept this [logic]; because what is obligatory upon them is to bring faith into existence instead of disbelief; as for bringing it into existence while disbelief persists, it is impossible. So it was possible for him to do, by the capacity that is suitable for both opposites, faith and not disbelief. So likewise these ones (the Mu'tazila), thus the objection on this basis is invalid.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection from the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that capacity is simultaneous with the act. The objection is that if capacity is not prior, then the unbeliever has no capacity for faith at the moment of unbelief, so commanding him to believe is an impossible obligation. The author answers by distinguishing two views on capacity. The first view, held by some of our scholars (i.e., Maturidis), is that the same capacity can be used for either opposite (belief or unbelief). On this view, the unbeliever's capacity for unbelief was also suitable for faith, so he was capable of believing but chose unbelief. Hence, the command is valid. The second view is that of the Mu'tazila themselves. They are forced to say that the obligation on the unbeliever is to produce faith instead of unbelief. But this is only possible if the capacity can be used for either opposite, which they deny. Thus, their own position leads to inconsistency. The author concludes that the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
قدرة تصلح للضدين
إيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's objection is unanswerable because if capacity is simultaneous, the unbeliever truly has no capacity for faith.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the same capacity that produces unbelief is also capable of producing faith, so the command is not impossible.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi refute the Mu'tazila's claim that simultaneous capacity would lead to obligation of the impossible?
A) He denies that capacity is simultaneous.
B) He argues that the capacity for disbelief is also suitable for faith.
C) He claims the unbeliever has no capacity.
D) He says God does not command faith.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi response to the Mu'tazila's objection shows that capacity can be suitable for opposite acts, so the command to believe is never an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a situation where you know you should obey God but feel it's too difficult, do you ever think, 'God is asking too much of me'? After studying this lesson, how does the fact that you have a capacity suitable for both obedience and disobedience change your excuse? What is one specific act of obedience you have been avoiding that you now realize you have no excuse for?
?
The Mu'tazila believed that God's command to the unbeliever is unjust if capacity is simultaneous. Are there any commands of God in your life that you have secretly thought were unfair or beyond your ability? This lesson says the capacity is there—you are choosing not to use it. Which command will you stop rationalizing away and start acting on today?
?
Nasafi refutes the objection by showing that the same capacity could have been used for faith instead of disbelief. Think of a time you blamed your circumstances or your nature for a sin. Did you have the capacity to choose otherwise? What does that realization demand from you right now?

Capacity Suitable for Opposites: Introduction and First Proof
Nasafi introduces the proofs and presents the first argument based on means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from means (asbab) that those who hold capacity is suitable for opposites use to support their position.

Core Idea
If tools can produce opposite actions, then capacity itself must be suitable for opposites.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi sets the stage for presenting the proofs of the Maturidi position that capacity (istita'a) is before the act and is suitable for opposite actions. He begins by recalling the earlier discussion and then introduces the first proof based on the nature of causes and instruments. This argument draws an analogy from physical tools to the capacity that operates through them.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction
Read the passage from 'ثم إنا لما سبق' to 'لنقف عليه' and understand Nasafi's purpose: to briefly present the proofs of each group.
Notice the phrase 'على طريقة الاختصار' indicating conciseness.
2
Identify the first proof
Locate the claim that every cause (sabab) is suitable for opposites, and the example of the tongue.
The proof is labeled as 'أدلة الذين يقولون...'.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider how the example of the tongue supports the claim: if the instrument can be used for opposite acts, then the capacity that uses it must also be able to produce opposite acts.
This is an analogical argument from tools to capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا لما سبق منا القول في كيفية الخلاف بين القائلين أن الاستطاعة الفعل في كون القدرة صالحة للضدين فلابد من أن نبين ها هنا لبعض ما يتعلق به كل فريق على طريقة الاختصار ليقف عليه قارئ كتابنا هذا فنقول :

[أدلة الذين يقولون إن الاستطاعة صالحة للضدين]:

إن القائلين بأنها تصلح للضدين يتعلقون بأشياء منها أن كل سبب من أسباب الفعل يصلح للضدين، فإن الآلات والأدوات المعدة لتتسم القدرة الناقصة صالحة للضدين كاللسان صلح للصدق والكذب والإقرار والتكذيب وغير ذلك، وكذا
Then, since we have previously spoken about the nature of the disagreement between those who say that capacity (istita'a) is before the act and those who say it is with the act, concerning whether capacity is suitable for opposites, we must now explain here some of what each group relies upon, by way of conciseness, so that the reader of our book may grasp it. We say:

[Proofs of those who say capacity is suitable for opposites]:

Indeed, those who say it is suitable for opposites cling to things; among them is that every cause (sabab) of an action is suitable for opposites. For the instruments and tools prepared to... the deficient capacity are suitable for opposites, like the tongue, which is suitable for truth and falsehood, affirmation and denial, and other such things. And likewise...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to a new section presenting the proofs of those who hold that capacity is suitable for opposites. The author states that he will briefly outline the arguments of each group. He then begins with the first proof of those who advocate for bi-directional capacity. The proof is that every cause (sabab) of an action is inherently suitable for opposite actions. He gives the example of instruments and tools, like the tongue, which can be used for truth or falsehood, affirmation or denial. This suggests that the capacity inhering in these tools is not restricted to one action but can produce opposing outcomes. The argument implies that if the tools are capable of opposites, then the capacity that uses them should also be capable of opposites. The passage cuts off at the end of the page, but it sets up the logical basis for the claim.

Key Terms

سبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That capacity being suitable for opposites means it can produce two actions simultaneously.
Correction: It means it can produce one of them at the agent's choice, not both at once.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The argument from instruments shows that if physical tools can be used for opposing actions, then the spiritual capacity that employs them must also be suitable for opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author argues that instruments are neutral and suitable for opposites. What instrument in your life (phone, money, time) are you using in a way that contradicts your faith? What would it mean to truly choose the good with it starting today?
?
If capacity is suitable for opposites, then every action you perform is a choice between good and evil. What does that say about your prayer? About your speech? Are you exercising your capacity to worship or to disobey? Write down one specific change you will make tonight to align your use of capacity with obedience to Allah.

Disagreement Among the Qadariyya on Capability
Nasafi notes Qadariyya confusion and cites al-Shahham's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) did not have a unified position on how capability is fit for opposites, and you will be introduced to Abu Ya'qub al-Shahham's specific view that power over a thing extends to its genus and opposite.

Core Idea
Even the Mu'tazila disagreed on the nature of capability.

Nasafi highlights a point often missed: the Qadariyya themselves were not of one mind on the precise nature of capability. He uses the strong term 'takhabbatat'—they became confused—to describe their internal debates. The passage then singles out the view of Abu Ya'qub al-Shahham, a prominent Mu'tazilī scholar, to illustrate one particular position.

The passage cuts off, but this fragment shows a key point of contention.

Mu'tazila Disagreements on Power's Capacity for Opposites
Author summarizes three Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) opinions on power and opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main Mu'tazila positions on whether power is capable of opposites, and how the author dismisses them as corrupt.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila disagreed even among themselves on power's scope over opposites.

The Mu'tazila (Qadariyya) were known for their rationalist approach, but they were far from unanimous. This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla presents three conflicting Mu'tazila views on the question of whether a single power can be effective over opposites. As you read, notice how each position differs in how broadly it defines power's scope, and how the author concludes that these opinions stem from corrupt principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Pay attention to the three distinct opinions marked by 'زعم' (claimed).
The author uses a dismissive tone, calling their views 'repugnant' (مستشنعة).
2
Identify the three views
Identify the three views: (1) Abu Ya'qub al-Shahham: power over a thing is over its entire genus and opposite, and God has power over servants' acts. (2) Majority: power over something is over some opposites but not all, and God and servant have distinct objects of power. (3) Later scholars: a single power can produce an infinite succession of acts but cannot do two same-genus acts simultaneously.
Notice the key terms: جنس (genus), ضد (opposite), قدرة (power).
3
Analyze the implications
Consider how each view impacts the relationship between divine and human power. Which view seems most restrictive? Which expands God's power? The author's critique unifies them as stemming from corrupt principles.
4
Reflect on the author's stance
Note that the author, Imam al-Nasafi (Maturidi), does not engage in detailed rebuttal here but simply labels these views as 'repugnant'. This indicates that from an orthodox Sunni perspective, all three are unacceptable.
This passage is a summary of sectarian differences, not a full argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تخبطت القدرية في كيفية وجوب كون القدرة صالحة للضدين، فزعم أبو يعقوب الشحام أن القدرة على الشيء قدرة على جميع جنسه وضده، وأن القديم جل وعلا قادر على أفعال عباده، وأن العبد إذا قدر على جنس من الفعل قدر على ما يقدر عليه ربه من أضداده. وزعم جمهورهم أن قدرة الإنسان على شيء قدرة على أضداد له لا يعم جنس الأضداد، وأن الباري جل وعلا لا يقدر على ما يقدر عليه عبده، ولا العبد يقدر على ما يقدر عليه ربه، وإن كان مقدور كل واحد منهما ضد مقدوره أو جنسه. وزعم بعض متأخريهم أن القدرة الواحدة قدرة على جميع ما يصح أن يكون يصح أن يفعل بها إلا واحدًا من الجنس، فإذا وجدت له أنه لا مقدورا له غير القدرة [؟] قدر بها على الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق والعلم والجهل والإرادة ثم يقول: إنها قدرة على أن يفعل بها من كل جنس ما لا يتناهى على التوالي وليست هي قدرة على أن يفعل بها في الحال شيئين من جنس واحد في محل واحد. ولهم وراء ذلك أقاويل مستشنعة دفعتهم إليها أصولهم الفاسدة
Then the Qadariyya stumbled regarding the manner of the necessity of power being capable of opposites. Abu Ya'qub al-Shahham claimed that power over a thing is power over all its genus and its opposite, and that the Ancient, Exalted is Powerful over the actions of His servants, and that the servant, if he has power over a genus of an act, has power over what his Lord has power over of its opposites. The majority of them claimed that a human's power over something is power over opposites of it that does not encompass the genus of opposites, and that the Creator, Exalted, has no power over what His servant has power over, nor does the servant have power over what his Lord has power over, even if the object of power of each one is the opposite of the object of power of the other or its genus. Some of their later ones claimed that a single power is power over all that it is valid that it be valid to do with it except one of the genus. Then if [?] exists for him that there is no object of power for him other than power [?], he has power by it over motion and stillness, gathering and separation, knowledge and ignorance, and will, then he says: it is a power to do with it from every genus what is infinite successively, and it is not a power to do with it at the moment two things of one genus in one locus. And besides that, they have repugnant statements to which their corrupt principles drove them.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage classifies the various Qadariyya (Mu'tazila) opinions on whether power (qudra) is capable of opposites. The author presents three views: (1) Abu Ya'qub al-Shahham: power over a thing is power over its entire genus and its opposite, and God has power over servants' actions. (2) Majority of Mu'tazila: power over something is power over its opposites but not all opposites, and God and servant have different objects of power. (3) Later Mu'tazila: a single power can produce an infinite succession of actions but cannot produce two in one locus simultaneously. The author dismisses these as repugnant and derived from corrupt principles.

Ash'ari and al-Isfarayini on Obligating the Impossible
Two responses to the charge of obligating the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two theological responses to the problem of obligating the impossible, particularly al-Ash'ari's argument from divine sovereignty and al-Isfarayini's distinction between impossibility and ugliness.

Core Idea
God's sovereignty makes obligating the impossible permissible; but true obligation requires capacity.

In this passage, the author presents how two Sunni theologians, al-Ash'ari and al-Isfarayini, address the Mu'tazilite charge that believing power cannot accommodate two opposites leads to obligating the impossible. Their responses reveal different foundational principles.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the problem
Understand that if power is not for opposites, then obligating one opposite when occupied with the other is obligating the impossible.
This is the background for the two responses.
2
Read al-Ash'ari's response
Notice how he appeals to God's absolute ownership and transcendence of benefit/harm to permit obligating the impossible in principle.
3
Examine al-Isfarayini's incomplete argument
See that he rejects obligating the impossible but bases it on the impossibility of the meaning of obligation with incapacity.
The argument is incomplete in the text.
4
Compare the approaches
Contrast the two: one permits it in principle but restricts by revelation, the other deems it intrinsically impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن المذهب الأول يندفع عنه هذا التشنيع الذي تتمسك به المعتزلة وهو نسبة قولنا: إن الاستطاعة الفعل إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق، وأما على مع المذهب الثاني وهو مذهب من يقول : إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين. فقد اختلفت عباراتهم في دفع هذا السؤال، فذكر الأشعري في كتابه المسمى بالنوادر أن تكليف ما لا يطاق جائز، وأن الله تعالى لو أمر عبده بالجمع بين الضدين لم يكن سفها ولا مستحيلاً، وهذا على أصله مستقيم فإن من أصله أن الله تعالى لو عذب الخلق في النيران خالدًا مخلدًا من غير جناية وجدت منهم كان ذلك حكمة وصوابًا؛ لأنه يتصرف في ملكه وكون تكليف ما لا يطاق في الشاهد إنما كان قبيحا لأن المكلف لا يتصرف في ملك نفسه، ولأن المكلف في الشاهد يأمر لجلب نفع وينهى لدفع ضرر، ولا يحصل ذلك بتكليف ما ليس في الوسع بخلاف الصانع جل جلاله فإنه يَجِلَّ عن جلب نفع أو دفع ضرر، ولا استحقاق من العبد لا للجنة ولا للنار بفعله وإنما الطاعة والمعصية أمارتان يستدل بهما، أن الله تعالى يعذب هذا في النار ويدخل ذلك الجنة، فعلى هذا لو كلف العبد ما لا يطاق ويجعل ذلك أمارة أنه معاقب كان ذلك جائزاً، وإذا كان كذلك كان تكليف العبد ما ليست له قدرة عليه لاشتغاله بضده جائزاً غير أن الشرع ورد بتكليف هذا النوع، ولم يرد بتكليف ذلك النوع مع استوائهما في الجواز. وقال أبو إسحاق الأسفرائيني: قال أهل الحق يستحيل تكليف ما لا يطاق لاستحالة وجود المعنى الذي يقتضيه التكليف مع العجز لا للقبح والسفه، وهذا لأن الذي يقتضيه التكليف استحقاق نوع من العقوبة على ضرب من المخالفة، وهذا
Then indeed, the first school is freed from this criticism which the Mu'tazila hold, namely attributing our statement that 'capacity is the act' to the saying of obligating what cannot be done. As for the second school, which is the school of those who say that power is not suitable for the two opposites, their expressions have differed in repelling this question. Al-Ash'ari mentioned in his book called al-Nawadir that obligating what cannot be done is permissible, and that if Allah the Exalted commanded His servant to combine between the two opposites, it would not be foolishness nor impossible. And this is consistent with his principle, for from his principle is that if Allah the Exalted were to torment the creatures in the fires eternally, without any crime committed by them, that would be wisdom and correct; because He acts in His dominion. And the fact that obligating what cannot be done in the witnessed realm is only ugly because the obligator does not act in his own dominion, and because the obligator in the witnessed realm commands to bring benefit and forbids to repel harm, and that is not achieved by obligating what is beyond capacity, unlike the Maker, glory be to His majesty, for He is exalted above bringing benefit or repelling harm. And there is no entitlement from the servant, neither for Paradise nor for Hellfire, by his action; rather, obedience and disobedience are two signs by which it is inferred that Allah the Exalted will torment this one in Hellfire and admit that one into Paradise. On this basis, if He obligated the servant with what he cannot do and made that a sign that he is to be punished, that would be permissible. And if that is so, then obligating the servant with what he has no power over due to being occupied with its opposite is permissible, except that the Law has come with obligating this type, and has not come with obligating that type, despite their equality in permissibility. And Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini said: The people of truth say that obligating what cannot be done is impossible due to the impossibility of the existence of the meaning that obligation necessitates along with incapacity, not due to ugliness and foolishness. And this is because what obligation necessitates is deserving of a type of punishment for a type of violation. And this...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage discusses two schools of thought regarding whether power can be for two opposites. The first school (that power is suitable for both) avoids the Mu'tazilite criticism of alleging obligating the impossible. The second school (that power is not for two opposites) faces the problem: if a person is occupied with one opposite, he lacks power for the other, so obligating the other would be obligating the impossible. The author presents two responses. First, al-Ash'ari's view: obligating the impossible is permissible (ja'iz). He argues from God's absolute sovereignty—God acts in His own dominion and is not bound by human considerations of benefit or harm. In the witnessed realm, obligation is only for benefit, but God transcends that. Obedience and disobedience are mere signs indicating divine reward or punishment, not causes of entitlement. Therefore, obligating something impossible can be a sign of punishment and is permissible. However, the revealed Law came only with obligations within capacity, not because the other is impossible. Second, Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini's view: obligating the impossible is impossible (mustahil) not because it is ugly or foolish, but because the very meaning of obligation (necessitating deserved punishment for violation) cannot coexist with incapacity. The text ends with the beginning of his reasoning, noting that obligation entails deserving punishment for violation, but the argument is incomplete.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
استطاعة
قدرة
ضدين
الأشعري
الإسفرائيني
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's view is irrational or arbitrary.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari's position is based on a coherent theological framework: God's absolute dominion and the sign-nature of human acts. He does not deny that obligating the impossible is ordinarily considered ugly; rather, he argues that God is not subject to the same criteria as human obligators.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, why is obligating what cannot be done not considered foolish or impossible?
A) Because God can do anything, including making the impossible possible
B) Because God is not seeking benefit or repelling harm, unlike human obligators
C) Because the servant always has the ability to choose
D) Because it is only a test

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari permits obligating the impossible based on God's sovereignty and the sign-nature of acts, while al-Isfarayini rejects it as conceptually impossible due to the meaning of obligation requiring capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ash'ari says our obedience and disobedience are mere signs, not causes of reward or punishment. Does this humbling truth make you more reliant on God's grace, or does it tempt you to apathy? Be honest: which response do you see in your heart right now, and what will you do to align it with the truth?
?
Al-Isfarayini insists that God never obligates beyond our capacity. When you face a command that feels impossible—like forgiving an unforgivable wrong or abandoning a deep-seated sin—do you truly believe that He has given you the capacity to obey? If not, what would it take for you to trust His promise?
?
Both scholars agree that God is sovereign over His creation, but they differ on whether obligating the impossible is permissible or impossible. How does your understanding of God's sovereignty affect the way you approach His commands in your daily life—with confidence that He is just and able, or with fear that He might ask more than you can bear? What needs to change in your soul?

Refutation of al-Ka'bi on Taklif Ma La Yutaq
Nasafi reports and refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that imposing what is unbearable is rationally evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that imposing what is unbearable is rationally evil, and why the notion of capacity must be understood correctly.

Core Idea
Imposing what is unbearable is only evil in a limited view of capacity.

Imam al-Maturidi engages with a key objection from al-Ka'bi regarding the obligation of acts before the time of action. Al-Ka'bi argued that such obligation is tantamount to imposing what is unbearable, which is rationally evil. In this passage, Nasafi reports al-Maturidi's concise refutation, showing that the concept of 'capacity' is broader than mere physical health, and that the alleged evil depends on a flawed understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how al-Ka'bi's objection is stated and then the response begins.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير قوة الظاهر'.
2
Identify the key argument
Focus on how al-Maturidi distinguishes between different understandings of 'tâqa' (capacity). He says al-Ka'bi's intellect only recognizes apparent strength (health) as capacity, while true capacity is broader.
The example of Moses' companion illustrates that Allah can command what He knows the person cannot bear in one sense, yet it remains valid.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this refutation defends the possibility of obligations that become binding only upon the acquisition of capacity, and how it preserves the justice of Allah's commands.
This connects to the larger Maturidi defense of taklif before capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعبر الشيخ أبو منصور رحمة الله عن هذه المعاني بأوجز لفظ وأحسن عبارة فحكى إلزام الكعبي أن ما قلتم تكليف ما لا يطاق وهو قبيح في العقل ثم أجاب فقال: إنما هو في العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير قوة الظاهر، وهي الصحة، فأما غيره فليس كما تقول بل كلف الله تعالى ثم صاحب موسى بما يعلم أنه لا يستطيع. ثم يقال له: وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل، والذي ادعيته من القبح إنما هو في عقل من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة وقت الفعل، فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح في العقل إن صدق فيما ادعى.
And Shaykh Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) expressed these meanings with the most concise expression and best phrasing. He reported the objection of al-Ka'bi that what you say is imposing what is unbearable (taklif ma la yutaq) which is evil in the intellect. Then he answered: It is only in the intellect that does not know capacity except as apparent strength, which is health. As for other than that, it is not as you say. Rather, Allah imposed upon the companion of Moses what He knew he could not bear. Then it is said to him: Likewise, imposing what is unbearable at the time of the act is evil in the intellect, but what you claim as evil is only in an intellect that deems the existence of the act and the power at the time of the act impossible. So his statement, upon analysis, is that which is evil in the intellect, if he is truthful in what he claims.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage reports al-Ka'bi's objection that the obligation of Hajj and jihad without immediate ability amounts to 'taklif ma la yutaq' (imposing what is beyond capacity), which is considered rationally evil. Abu Mansur counters that this notion of 'tâqa' (capacity) is limited to the apparent strength (quwwa al-zahir) meaning physical health, but true capacity is broader. Allah imposed on Moses' companion what He knew he could not bear, yet it was a valid command. Furthermore, even if one accepts that imposing what is unbearable at the time of action is evil, al-Ka'bi's own intellect that deems existence of act and power simultaneously impossible is the one that finds it evil; but that is a false premise.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
قبح
طاقة
قوة الظاهر
صحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taklif ma la yutaq is always rationally evil regardless of how capacity is understood.
Correction: The passage shows it is only evil in an intellect that limits capacity to apparent strength (health) and denies the possibility of power at the time of action.

Checkpoint
How does Abu Mansur respond to al-Ka'bi's claim that taklif ma la yutaq is rationally evil?
A) He agrees and says it is evil only for Allah
B) He says it is only evil in an intellect that knows only apparent strength
C) He says it is not evil because Allah can do what He wills
D) He says it is evil but necessary

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by showing that 'capacity' is not limited to physical health and that the alleged evil of imposing what is unbearable depends on a flawed intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that seems beyond your current ability, do you trust His knowledge of your capacity more than your own limited assessment? What would change if you truly believed that His understanding of your limits is greater than yours?
?
Have you ever judged an obligation as 'unreasonable' or 'impossible' because it appeared too difficult, without considering that your definition of 'ability' might be too narrow? In what area of your life right now might you be doing this?
?
Al-Ka'bi's intellect was limited by its own assumptions. How often do you rely on your own intellect to declare something 'unfair' in your life, forgetting that Allah's wisdom is beyond your comprehension? What would it mean to surrender that judgment today?

The Jabriyya View on Human Actions
Nasafi states the Jabriyya view that all actions are from Allah and humans have no power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jabriyya position that all human actions are from Allah alone and humans have no real power, and their view that attributing actions to humans is metaphorical.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya reduce human actions to involuntary movements, denying human agency.

This lesson introduces a key sectarian position in Islamic theology: the Jabriyya (determinists). Imam Nasafi presents their view that all actions of creation are directly from Allah, and that humans have no power or management over their deeds. This sets the stage for the Maturidi response affirming human free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Nasafi describes the Jabriyya view and the specific comparisons used.
The passage uses a comparison to involuntary movements like tremors and pulses.
2
Identify the core claim
What is the central belief of the Jabriyya? They attribute all actions to Allah and deny human power entirely.
This is the key theological point.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
Note the example: saying 'Zaid went' is like saying 'the boy grew tall' – an action attributed to the subject but not actually performed by them.
This shows the Jabriyya's hermeneutic.
4
Recognize the sect attribution
The view is attributed to Jahm ibn Safwan. This positions the Jabriyya within early Islamic sectarian debates.
You will later see how Maturidiyya responds to this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف الناس في أفعال الخلق جعلها بعضهم الله تعالى، ونفوا عنها تدبير الخلق وأزالوا عنها قدرتهم بل لم يثبتوا لهم قدرة، وجعلوها كلها اضطرارية كحركات المرتعش وحركات العروق النابضة، وأحالوا اتصاف العباد بالقدرة (2) وهو قول جهم (3) بن صفوان الترمذي، وإضافتها إلى العباد عند هؤلاء مجاز، فيكون قول القائل : ذهب زيد و مشى عمرو بمنزلة قول القائل طال الغلام ومات زيد وأبيض شعر عمر وشاخ عبد الله (4).
The people differed concerning the actions of creation: some of them attributed them to Allah, negated from them the management of creation, removed from them their power, rather they did not affirm for them any power, and made them all compulsory like the movements of a tremor sufferer and the movements of pulsating veins, and they deemed it impossible for servants to be described with power (2) – and this is the saying of Jahm (3) ibn Safwan al-Tirmidhi – and attributing them to the servants according to these people is metaphorical, so the saying of the speaker: 'Zaid went' and 'Amr walked' is like the saying of the speaker: 'The boy grew tall', 'Zaid died', 'Umar's hair turned white', and 'Abdullah grew old' (4).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage states the Jabriyya position on human actions. The author presents their view: some people attributed all actions of creation to Allah alone, negating any human agency or power. They compared human actions to involuntary movements like shivering or pulsing veins, thus denying that humans are truly capable of action. For them, phrases like 'Zaid went' are metaphorical, similar to saying 'the boy grew tall' where the change is not an act of the subject but a natural occurrence. The passage ends by attributing this view to Jahm ibn Safwan al-Tirmidhi and noting that for these people, attributing actions to servants is purely metaphorical.

Key Terms
الجبر
جهم بن صفوان
اضطرارية
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Jabriyya completely deny human actions; they affirm actions but attribute them to Allah metaphorically, not denying the actions themselves.
Correction: The Jabriyya do not deny that actions occur; they deny that humans have any efficacy or power in producing them.

Checkpoint
How do the Jabriyya characterize human actions according to Nasafi?
A) As voluntary and created by humans
B) As involuntary movements like tremors and pulses
C) As partly from Allah and partly from humans
D) As disconnected from Allah's power

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya view reduces human actions to involuntary, divinely compelled events, denying human power and treating action attributions as metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'it was my fate' or 'Allah wrote it' to avoid feeling guilty? How does the Jabriyya view (and its refutation) challenge that excuse?
?
If you truly believed every action of yours is like a tremor or a heartbeat—completely involuntary—would you be able to repent? What does that tell you about the necessity of your own will in your relationship with Allah?
?
Consider one decision you made today. Can you honestly say you had real choice in it, or are you tempted to attribute it to circumstances? What is at stake if you deny your own role in that action?

The Mu'tazila View on Human Actions
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila view that humans create their own actions, including its historical development.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine of human action creation and its historical evolution from cautious terminology to extreme claims.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila progressively escalated their claim that humans create their own actions, eventually denying Allah's creative role.

In this passage, Nasafi outlines the Mu'tazila position on human actions, showing how their views evolved over time. Early Mu'tazila were cautious, but later figures like Abu Ali al-Jubbai and al-Ja'd took more extreme positions. By tracing this development, we see the sect's departure from mainstream Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall narrative. Notice the key figures and terminology shifts.
Focus on the historical progression mentioned.
2
Identify the three stages
The passage describes three phases: the early Mu'tazila, Abu Ali al-Jubbai, and al-Ja'd. Identify what each group/individual claimed about human action and divine creation.
Early Mu'tazila avoided calling the servant a 'creator'; al-Jubbai did; al-Ja'd denied Allah's true creation.
3
Examine the terminology
Note the shift from using 'ijad' and 'ihdath' (invention/origination) to 'takhliq' (creation). Understand why this was significant.
The term 'creator' (khalik) is reserved for Allah in mainstream Islam.
4
Reflect on the author's tone
Observe how Nasafi criticizes the Mu'tazila, especially al-Ja'd. This shows the gravity of the divergence from consensus.
The phrase 'exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say' underscores the condemnation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعضهم جعلوها العباد وقطعوا تدبير الله عنها بالكلية، وقالوا: يخترعها العباد فيخرجونها من العدم إلى الوجود ويحدثونها، ويقولون: إيجادها وإحداثها شاء الله تعالى ذلك أم لم يشأ، وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة القدرية بأجمعهم، إلا أن أوائلهم كانوا يتحرجون عن إطلاق لفظة التخليق وتسمية العبد خالقا (1)، وكانوا يطابقون جميع المسلمين في قولهم: لا خالق إلا الله وإن كانوا يثبتون معنى الخلق كانوا يطلقون لفظة الإيجاد والإحداث، ويسمون العبد موجدًا محدثًا إلى زمن أبي علي الجبائي، فلما رأى معنى الخلق ثابتًا أطلق لفظة الخالق (1) وسمي العبد خالقًا، ولم يبال من مخالفة إجماع الأمة مع إقراره بكونه حجة موجبة للعلم قطعًا كالنص لما رأى من تحير وعجزهم عن التفرقة بين الإيجاد والتخليق، وكان الناس يتعجبون من جرأته على الله تعالى وركوبه حطة (2) مخالفة إجماع المسلمين ليتمكن من تحقيق ضلالاته إلى أن نشأ أبو عبد الله (3) البصري (4) المعروف بالجعد منهم وأتى بما لم يتجاسر عليه مشرك، فزعم أن الله تعالى ليس بخالق فى الحقيقة بل يوصف بذلك مجازا وإنما الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا (6).
And some of them attributed [actions] to the servants and completely cut off Allah's management from them, and said: the servants invent them, bringing them from non-existence to existence, and cause them to occur, and say: their invention and occurrence [happen] whether Allah wills that or not. And the Mu'tazila Qadariyya all went to this, except that their early ones were reluctant to using the term 'creation' and calling the servant a creator (1), and they agreed with all Muslims in their saying 'there is no creator but Allah', even though they affirmed the meaning of creation; they would use the term 'invention' and 'origination', and call the servant an inventor and an originator until the time of Abu Ali al-Jubbai. When he saw that the meaning of creation is established, he used the term 'creator' (1) and called the servant a creator, and he did not care about opposing the consensus of the Ummah while acknowledging that it is a proof necessarily producing knowledge like the text, because he saw their confusion and inability to distinguish between invention and creation. And people were astonished at his audacity against Allah and his undertaking a great matter (2) of opposing the consensus of Muslims in order to realize his misguidances, until Abu Abdullah (3) al-Basri (4) known as al-Ja'd arose among them and presented what no polytheist had dared: he claimed that Allah is not a creator in reality but is described as such metaphorically, and the actual creator is the servant – exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, greatly (6).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position on human actions. The author begins by stating that some people attribute actions entirely to the servants, cutting off Allah's management. They claim humans invent actions independently, whether Allah wills or not. The author notes that all Mu'tazila Qadariyya held this view, but with a historical evolution: the early ones avoided the word 'creation' (takhliq) and calling the servant a creator, instead using 'invention' (ijad) and 'origination' (ihdath). They called the servant an inventor and originator. This changed with Abu Ali al-Jubbai, who saw the meaning of creation as established and therefore used 'creator' for the servant, disregarding the consensus of the Ummah. The author criticizes this audacity. Then al-Ja'd went further, claiming that Allah is not truly a creator but only metaphorically, and the real creator is the servant. The author ends with a condemnation.

Misconception: That all Mu'tazila held the same extreme view from the beginning.
Correction: The passage shows an evolution: early Mu'tazila were cautious and avoided calling the servant a creator, while later figures like Abu Ali al-Jubbai and al-Ja'd increasingly deviated.

Checkpoint

B) He began calling the servant a 'creator' explicitly
C) He denied that the servant has any power
D) He claimed that Allah is not a creator in any sense

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view on human actions developed from cautious terminology to outright denial of Allah's creative role, with al-Ja'd claiming Allah is only metaphorically creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there any small 'terminological shifts' you have made regarding Allah's attributes or your own abilities that could gradually lead you away from tawheed? How will you guard against that starting today?
?
The early Mu'tazila thought they were safe by avoiding the word 'creator', yet their affirmation of the meaning opened the door to greater error. Is there any belief you hold that outwardly looks orthodox but inwardly contradicts the consensus of the Salaf? Will you investigate and correct it now?
?
Al-Ja'd claimed Allah is only metaphorically the creator—an extreme few would openly say. But do you ever, in moments of pride or self-reliance, act as if you are the true creator of your achievements? What verse or hadith will you recite whenever you feel that tendency arise?

Mu'tazila's Use of 'Khaliq' and al-Ja'd's Extremism
Historical narrative of Mu'tazila's attribution of creation to humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical progression of Mu'tazila thought on attributing creative power to humans, from cautious terms to the extreme claim that the servant is the true creator.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila escalated from 'originator' to 'creator' for humans; al-Ja'd claimed only servants create.

This lesson traces a troubling development within Mu'tazila theology. What began as cautious language about human agency ended with an explicit claim that Allah is not the real creator. We will follow this shift from early terms to al-Jubba'i's bold step, and finally to al-Ja'd's extreme assertion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the historical narrative. Pay attention to the specific terms used.
2
Identify the stages of escalation
Notice the progression: earlier Mu'tazila used 'mujid' and 'muhdith'; al-Jubba'i then called the servant 'khaliq'; al-Ja'd went further by denying that Allah is truly the creator.
3
Consider the violation of consensus
The text says al-Jubba'i disregarded ijmā' despite acknowledging it as a definitive proof. Ask yourself why he would do this and what it reveals about his methodology.
4
Reflect on al-Ja'd's extreme claim
Al-Ja'd claimed that Allah is not the real creator but only metaphorically, and that the true creator is the servant. This is a radical departure from orthodox Islamic belief.
The passage ends with a Quranic condemnation.
5
Connect to orthodox response
The author concludes with the Quranic verse, showing the gravity of such statements. Consider why the consensus of the Ummah is important in preserving correct belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظة الإيجاد والإحداث، ويسمون العبد موجدًا محدثًا إلى زمن أبي علي الجبائي، فلما رأى معنى الخلق ثابتًا أطلق لفظة الخالق وسمي العبد خالقًا، ولم يبال من مخالفة إجماع الأمة مع إقراره بكونه حجة موجبة للعلم قطعًا كالنص لما رأى من تحير وعجزهم عن التفرقة بين الإيجاد والتخليق، وكان الناس يتعجبون من جرأته على الله تعالى وركوبه حطة مخالفة إجماع المسلمين ليتمكن من تحقيق ضلالاته إلى أن نشأ أبو عبد الله البصري المعروف بالجعد منهم وأتى بما لم يتجاسر عليه مشرك، فزعم أن الله تعالى ليس بخالق فى الحقيقة بل يوصف بذلك مجازا وإنما الخالق على الحقيقة هو العبد - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا.
The word of bringing into existence and originating, and they called the servant a bringer into existence and an originator until the time of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i. When he saw the meaning of creation established, he released the word 'creator' and called the servant a creator, and he did not care about violating the consensus of the Ummah while acknowledging it as a definitive proof like the text, because he saw their confusion and inability to differentiate between bringing into existence and creation. And people were amazed at his audacity against Allah and his undertaking a grave act of violating the consensus of Muslims to be able to realize his misguidances, until there arose Abu Abd Allah al-Basri known as al-Ja'd from among them, and he brought what no polytheist had dared: he claimed that Allah is not a creator in reality but is described as such metaphorically, and that the real creator is the servant - exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, greatly.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative tracing the radicalization of Mu'tazila thought regarding the attribution of creative power to human beings. It begins by noting that earlier Mu'tazila (before Abu Ali al-Jubba'i) used the terms 'bringer into existence' (mūjid) and 'originator' (muḥdith) for the servant, but avoided calling the servant 'creator' (khāliq). Then Abu Ali al-Jubba'i, seeing that the meaning of creation (khalq) was established in their usage, boldly applied the term 'creator' to the servant, disregarding the consensus (ijmā') of the Ummah, which he otherwise regarded as a definitive proof (ḥujja) equivalent to a text (naṣṣ). The author expresses amazement at his audacity. The narrative culminates with Abu Abd Allah al-Basri (known as al-Ja'd), who went even further: he claimed that Allah is not a creator in reality but only metaphorically, and that the true creator is the servant. The passage ends with a Quranic condemnation of such statements, showing the author's rejection of this extreme view.

إحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: This passage shows a progression of extremism: earlier Mu'tazila used cautious terms, then al-Jubba'i called the servant 'khaliq', and finally al-Ja'd claimed Allah is not the real creator.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's attribution of the term 'creator' to humans escalated until al-Ja'd denied Allah's true creatorship, showing the danger of departing from consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you attribute your own achievements or abilities to yourself as the ultimate cause, forgetting that Allah is the only true Creator? In what specific areas of your life do you need to repent from this subtle shirk?
?

?
Al-Ja'd claimed Allah is not the real Creator—a statement that seems obviously false, but do you live as if you are the independent creator of your own actions and outcomes? What does the truth that 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (Quran 39:62) demand of you starting now?

First Mu'tazili Proof from 'Best of Creators'
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazili proof from 'Best of Creators' verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazili proof for human creation from the Quranic phrase 'best of creators' and the logic behind their argument.

Core Idea
A comparative phrase implies multiple participants – that is the Mu'tazili hook.

One of the most famous Quranic verses cited by the Mu'tazila to argue that humans create their own actions is the phrase 'best of creators.' In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents their reasoning before later refuting it. We will examine how they derive their proof from comparative language and the analogy they use.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Focus on understanding the structure: first, the Mu'tazila cite the verse; then, they explain why it implies other creators.
Notice the contrast with the phrase 'greatest of gods' – that is key.
2
Identify the core argument
The Mu'tazila argue that the comparative 'أحسن' (best) requires at least two parties to compare. This implies that there must be other creators besides Allah for the phrase 'best of creators' to make sense.
3
Understand the analogy and contrast
They draw an analogy with 'most skilled of writers' to illustrate how comparatives work. Then they contrast with 'greatest of gods' which is impossible because there is no other god – showing that the Quran would not use such a phrase if other creators did not exist.
This contrast strengthens their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام بيننا وبين المعتزلة فنبدأ ببيان ما لهم من الشبه فنقول: إنهم يحتجون من كتاب الله تعالى كقوله تعالى: فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ، في الآية دلالة كون غيره خالقا لأن إطلاق ذلك يوجب أن له تعالى مزية على كل خالق كما يقال: فلان أحذق الكتاب وأجودهم خطًا. ألا ترى أن القول بأنه تعالى أكبر الآلهة محال لاستحالة ثبوت الإلهية لغيره، وهاهنا لما أطلق هذا دل أن غيره خالق.
As for the discourse between us and the Mu'tazila, we begin by presenting their specious arguments. We say: They argue from the Book of Allah, may He be exalted, such as His saying: 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators.' In the verse there is evidence that other than Him is a creator, because the application of this phrase necessitates that He, may He be exalted, has superiority over every creator, just as it is said: 'So-and-so is the most skilled of writers and the most excellent of them in calligraphy.' Do you not see that the statement that He, may He be exalted, is the greatest of gods is impossible due to the impossibility of divinity being established for other than Him, and here, when this was applied, it indicates that other than Him is a creator.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage begins a new section addressing the Mu'tazila. The author states that he will first present the Mu'tazila's specious arguments (شبه) before refuting them. The first proof they cite is from Quran 23:14: 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators' (فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ). The Mu'tazila argue that this phrase implies there are other creators, because the comparative 'best' (أحسن) requires multiple participants—otherwise one would not say 'best' if there were only one. They draw an analogy: if you say 'So-and-so is the most skilled of writers and the most excellent of them in calligraphy,' this implies there are other writers. Similarly, 'best of creators' implies there are other creators besides God. They contrast this with a phrase like 'the greatest of gods' (أكبر الآلهة), which would be impossible because there is no other god; thus such a phrase is not used. Here, since God used 'best of creators,' it indicates that others, namely humans, are also creators. This is the Mu'tazili argument; the author is merely presenting it and will rebut it later.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الشبه
أحسن الخالقين
الخالق
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author endorses the Mu'tazili interpretation because he presents it without immediate refutation.
Correction: Imam Nasafi is first presenting their argument (shubah) before refuting it. He does not endorse it.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila use the verse 'أحسن الخالقين' to argue for human creation?
A) They claim the verse proves God creates directly without intermediaries
B) They argue the comparative 'أحسن' implies existence of other creators
C) They say the verse negates any creation by humans
D) They interpret 'الخالقين' as angels, not humans

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the comparative adjective in 'best of creators' to argue that other creators must exist, namely humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'So blessed is Allah, the best of creators,' do you feel a sense of awe that God is superior to any creator, including yourself? In what specific areas of your life do you unconsciously claim a share of creativity that belongs only to Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila used a linguistic argument to prove that humans create their own actions. If you accept that interpretation, what would that mean for your personal accountability before Allah? Are you ready to bear full responsibility for every deed as if you are its sole creator?
?
This lesson presents an argument that you may disagree with. How do you respond when the Qur'anic text seems to allow a meaning that challenges your beliefs? Do you humbly seek deeper understanding, or do you rely on surface-level readings to support your own opinions?

beginner
4 min
Mu'tazila's Linguistic Argument from 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin'
Mu'tazila use the Qur'anic phrase 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' to argue that others create

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's linguistic argument from the Qur'anic verse 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' that other beings are creators.

Core Idea
A comparative adjective implies other participants in the category.

The Mu'tazila, a rationalist theological school, often used the Qur'an itself to argue for human capacity to create actions. One of their key proofs is the verse 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (23:14). At first glance, this verse simply praises God. But the Mu'tazila read it carefully: the word 'best' (ahsan) is a comparative, which only makes sense if there are other creators. In this lesson, we examine their reasoning and the linguistic principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Note the key phrase 'أحسن الخالقين'.
2
Identify the linguistic point
Recognize that 'ahsan' is a comparative adjective (ism tafdeel). In Arabic, a comparative implies a comparison between at least two entities.
3
Understand the analogy with 'best writer'
The Mu'tazila analogize: 'best of writers' only makes sense if there are other writers. Similarly, 'best of creators' only makes sense if there are other creators.
4
Contrast with 'greatest of gods'
They contrast this with 'greatest of gods', which is impossible because no other gods exist. This shows that the verse's form is meaningful only if other creators exist.
This is a key refutation of the Ash'ari objection that the phrase is simply hyperbolic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكلام بيننا وبين المعتزلة فنبدأ ببيان ما لهم من الشبه فنقول: إنهم يحتجون من كتاب الله تعالى كقوله تعالى : فَتَبَارَكَ اللَّهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ في الآية دلالة كون غيره خالقا لأن إطلاق ذلك يوجب أن له تعالى مزية على كل خالق كما يقال : فلان أحذق الكتاب وأجودهم خطًا . ألا ترى أن القول بأنه تعالى أكبر الآلهة محال لاستحالة ثبوت الإلهية لغيره، وهاهنا لما أطلق هذا دل أن غيره خالق.
Now, as for the discussion between us and the Mu'tazila, we begin by explaining what doubts they have, so we say: They argue from the Book of God Almighty, such as His saying: 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (23:14). In the verse there is evidence that other than Him is a creator, because the use of that expression necessitates that He Almighty has superiority over every creator, just as it is said: 'So-and-so is the most skillful of writers and the best of them in handwriting.' Do you not see that saying that He Almighty is the greatest of gods is impossible because divinity cannot be established for another? And here, since He used this expression, it indicates that other than Him is a creator.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila from the Qur'an. They cite the verse 'So blessed be God, the best of creators' (23:14). They argue that the word 'best' (أحسن) implies a comparative degree, which requires the existence of other creators over whom God is superior. They draw an analogy: if you say 'So-and-so is the most skillful of writers,' that implies there are other writers. Similarly, if it were impossible for others to create, then saying 'best of creators' would be like saying 'greatest of gods,' which is absurd because no other gods exist. Therefore, the Mu'tazila conclude that the verse proves that humans can create actions.

Key Terms
أحسن الخالقين
خالق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse simply praises God without implying other creators.
Correction: The Mu'tazila focus on the linguistic implication of the comparative form: a comparative adjective logically requires other members of the category to be meaningful.

Checkpoint
How do the Mu'tazila derive the existence of other creators from the verse 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin'?
A) They argue that 'ahsan' only makes sense if there are others to compare.
B) They claim the verse explicitly states that humans create.
C) They say the verse rejects any creation by others.
D) They interpret 'khaliq' as metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that the comparative 'ahsan' in 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' necessarily implies the existence of other creators, making the verse a proof for human ability to create actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Ahsan al-Khaliqin' in your prayers, do you ever pause to consider that this verse might imply other creators? How does that challenge or affirm your understanding of your own role in your actions? Will you reflect on this the next time you are tempted to claim full credit for a success?
?
If every action you perform is truly created by Allah alone, why do you feel pride or shame for your deeds? How does the Mu'tazila argument make you confront the tension between divine control and personal responsibility—and which side does your heart lean toward in moments of sin?
?
The Mu'tazila use language to prove a point. Are you using your own words and interpretations of Qur'anic verses to support your desires instead of submitting to the clear meaning? Search your heart: do you twist the text to justify your actions, or do you let the text transform you?
