The Fourfold Dilemma of 'Invention'
The author subjects the meaning of 'invention' to a fourfold logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi scholar uses a fourfold logical dilemma to expose the incoherence of the Mu'tazili concept of invention.

Core Idea
Invention cannot be a coherent object of power; it leads to contradiction.

The Mu'tazila claim that human power is 'over invention'. But what exactly is invention? The Maturidi author forces them to define it. He systematically examines every possible definition and shows that each contradicts their own principles. This passage is a masterclass in logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let the rhythm of the dilemma sink in.
Notice the four-fold structure: existent, non-existent, both, neither.
2
Identify the four possibilities
The author lists four possible definitions for 'invention'. Write them down as you find them in the text.
3
Observe how each is refuted
For each possibility, the author points out an impossibility based on Mu'tazili own principles. Track which principle is violated.
For 'existent': power cannot attach to existent. For 'non-existent': attributes in non-existence cannot be attached to power. For 'both': contradiction. For 'neither': inconceivable.
4
Understand the Bāṭiniyya comparison
The author compares the fourth possibility to the Bāṭiniyya who say God is neither existent nor non-existent, which leads to atheism and sophistry. This shows the danger of such a definition.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this dilemma undermines the Mu'tazili claim that human power is over invention. Is the concept of invention salvageable?
The lesson is that without a coherent definition, the claim is baseless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول ما معنى قولكم: إن قدرتنا قدرة على الاختراع؟ وما هذا الاختراع الذي تزعمون أن قدرتكم متعلقة به أهو اسم لموجود أم لمعدوم وموجود أم لا لموجود ولا لمعدوم؟. فإن قلتم : إنه اسم لموجود، فهو محال عندكم لأن تعلق القدرة بالموجود عندكم محال، وإن قلتم : إنه اسم لمعدوم، فهو محال أيضا، لأنه يوجب أن يكون في العدم اختراع وكل وصف كان في العدم ثابتا كان تعلق القدرة به محالاً كالشيئية والجوهر والعرضية والعينية وغير ذلك من الأوصاف الثابتة في العدم عندكم، وإن قلتم إنه اسم للموجود والمعدوم فهذا كلام متناقض، وإن قلتم إنه اسم لا لموجود ولا لمعدوم فهو اسم لما لا يعقل إذ لا يتصور معلوم ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، وهذا من جهالات الباطنية فإنهم يزعمون أن الباري عز وجل ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، وهذا قول يؤدي إلى الإلحاد والسفسطة، والله الموفق.
Then we say: What is the meaning of your statement: 'Our power is a power over invention'? And what is this invention which you claim your power is attached to? Is it a name for an existent or for a non-existent, or for both existent and non-existent, or for neither existent nor non-existent? For if you say: It is a name for an existent, then it is impossible according to you because attachment of power to the existent is impossible according to you. And if you say: It is a name for a non-existent, it is also impossible, because it necessitates that in non-existence there be invention, and every attribute that is established in non-existence, the attachment of power to it is impossible—like thing-ness, substance, being an accident, being an entity, and other attributes established in non-existence according to you. And if you say it is a name for the existent and the non-existent, then this is contradictory speech. And if you say it is a name for neither existent nor non-existent, then it is a name for what is inconceivable, for there is no conceivable known thing that is neither existent nor non-existent. And this is from the ignorances of the Bāṭiniyya, for they claim that the Creator, may He be exalted, is neither existent nor non-existent. And this statement leads to atheism and sophistry. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of the Mu'tazili concept of 'invention'. The Maturidi author asks what this invention is as a name. He presents four possibilities: it is a name for an existent, a non-existent, both, or neither. For each, he points out contradictions based on the Mu'tazila's own doctrines. If it is an existent, they claim power cannot attach to an existent. If non-existent, then invention would be in non-existence, which is impossible. If both, contradiction. If neither, then it is inconceivable, akin to the Bāṭiniyya who say God is neither existent nor non-existent, which leads to atheism. Thus, the Mu'tazili claim is incoherent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Invention is a self-evident concept that can be defined unambiguously.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that every attempt to define invention leads to contradiction, so it is not a coherent concept at all.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the possibilities considered for the meaning of 'invention'?
A) Name for an existent.
B) Name for a non-existent.
C) Name for a contingent being.
D) Name for neither existent nor non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili concept of 'invention' cannot be consistently defined, rendering their claim of power over invention logically untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author calls the view that something can be neither existent nor non-existent 'ignorance' leading to atheism. Search your own heart: have you ever held a belief about Allah or about reality that you could not logically define? Are you willing to abandon any incoherent thought, even if it feels comfortable? Write down one such belief you need to correct.
?
This passage forces the Mu‘tazila to define 'invention' and shows every definition fails. What areas of your own faith or practice have you left undefined, hoping they would 'just make sense'? Commit today to seeking clear, sound knowledge from the Qur’an and Sunnah, even if it means dismantling a cherished idea.
?
The author warns that a vague concept leads to sophistry (denial of truth). Think of a time when you used vague religious language to avoid a hard question. Will you now face that question with the clarity this lesson demands, or will you continue in ambiguity? Resolve to pursue truth over comfort.

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
5 min
Turning the Mu'tazili Objection Back
Response to Mu'tazili objection by turning it back.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response uses the Mu'tazila's own principles to reverse the objection about God creating motion.

Core Idea
The Maturidi turns the Mu'tazili question back by asking: what do you do, if God does not create the substance?

This passage presents a clever dialectical argument. A Mu'tazili objection is turned inside out: they ask 'if God creates motion, what do I do?' The author responds by showing that, according to their own denial of human power over existence, they cannot explain what the human does either. The counter-question becomes a proof for the Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: the objection and the response.
The argument is concise; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the objection and the counter-question
Locate the Mu'tazili objection: 'If Allah performs motion, what did I do?' Then find the author's counter-question: 'If you did not do the thing nor the entity, and God did not do the substance, accident, etc., then what did you do?'
The counter-question mirrors the structure of the objection.
3
Understand the logical twist
The author assumes the Mu'tazila's own principle: existence is identical to the entity, and God alone creates existence. So if God does not create the entities (substance, accident, etc.), neither does the human. Thus the Mu'tazila's answer to the counter-question also applies to their original objection.
This shows the objection is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الله تعالى إذا فعل الحركة فماذا فعلت أنا ؟ فيقال لهم وإذا لم تفعل أنت الشيء ولا العين والوجود هو عين الشيء إذ ليس بمعنى وراءه فماذا فعلت إذا لم يفعل الله الجوهر ولا العرض ولا الشيء ولا العين ولا السواد ولا البياض ولا الرائحة ولا الطعم فماذا فعل؟ ووجودها إذا لم يكن معنى وراءها أو هو عينها وذاتها ولم يفعل عينها ولا ذاتها فماذا فعل؟ فما أجابوا من شيء فهو لهم جواب.
And with this they are answered regarding their statement: 'If Allah performs motion, then what did I do?' So it is said to them: And if you did not do the thing nor the entity—and existence is the very entity of the thing since it is not a meaning beyond it—then what did you do if Allah did not do the substance, nor the accident, nor the thing, nor the entity, nor blackness, nor whiteness, nor odor, nor taste? So what did He do? And if their existence is not a meaning beyond them, or He is the very entity and essence of them, and He did not do their entity nor their essence, then what did He do? So whatever they answer to something, it is an answer for them.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage provides a response to a specific Mu'tazili objection: 'If God creates motion, what do I do?' The author turns the question back on them. He argues that according to the Mu'tazila's own denial that anything besides God has power over existence, and since existence is the same as the entity, then if God does not create the substance, accident, thing, entity, blackness, etc., then neither did the human. So the Mu'tazila cannot explain what the human allegedly does. Whatever they answer, the same logic applies to the Maturidi position.

Key Terms
الحركة
الشيء
العين
الوجود
الجوهر
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazili objection that 'if God creates motion, the human does nothing' is unanswerable.
Correction: The author answers by turning the question back, showing the Mu'tazila cannot explain human action either, given their own principles.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the Mu'tazili question, 'If God creates motion, what did I do?'
A) He agrees that the human does nothing.
B) He argues that the human's act is the motion itself.
C) He turns it back by asking what the human did if God did not create the substance and accident.
D) He says the question is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi response to the Mu'tazili objection about God creating motion is to reverse the question, showing the Mu'tazila cannot explain human action either.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly feel that God created that sin, or do you instinctively claim ownership of the act? How does this passage challenge your hidden assumption that some actions are entirely yours?
?
If God does not create the very entity of your good deeds, then what have you actually done? Can you point to any action in your life that is entirely your own creation? Let this question sit until it unsettles you.
?
The Mu'tazila claimed humans create their own acts to preserve divine justice. But the Maturidi turns their logic back: you create nothing at all. Does this make God unjust? Or does it reveal that your concept of justice needs to be redefined by the truth of God's absolute sovereignty?
